The Trilemma of the Preserved Tablet
Maturidi poses a trilemma about what is written in the Preserved Tablet regarding lifespan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's trilemma that exposes the logical consequences of asserting that God's decree in the Preserved Tablet is not conditional on human actions.

Core Idea
God's knowledge cannot be subject to contingent human choices without absurdity.

Maturidi presents a powerful trilemma to his opponents who claim that the Preserved Tablet records fixed destinies. He forces them to choose among three possibilities about the duration written in the Tablet, each leading to an unacceptable conclusion. This passage demonstrates the logical method of kalam: using reason to reveal contradictions in opposing views.

Guided Path
1
Read the dilemma
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Maturidi offers three options regarding what is written in the Preserved Tablet about a person's lifespan. Identify each option.
The options are: God keeps the person until a time, the person lasts until that time, or God keeps the person and he lasts if not killed.
2
Trace the consequences
After each option, Maturidi shows the consequence. For the first two, the opponent accuses God of falsehood or breach of promise. For the third, the question of God's knowledge arises.
Notice how Maturidi uses the opponent's own assumptions to trap them.
3
Reflect on the outcome
If the opponent says God did not know whether the person would be killed, that is blasphemy. If they say God knew, then writing a conditional statement in the Tablet is an act of ignorance. Thus, the opponent is left with no valid option.
This trilemma shows the flaw in separating God's decree from His knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له : ما ضرب من المدة له ألا كان فى اللوح أنه يبقيه إلى ذلك ، أو يبقى هو إلى ذلك ، أو يبقيه ويبقى إن لم يقتل ؟ فإن قال بالأول والثانى فادعى عليه الكذب فى خبره والخلف فى وعده ، وإن قال بالثالث قيل : أكان يعلم يقتل أو لا ؟ فإن قال : لا ، استحق الإبانة بين رأسه وجسده ، والخلود في عذاب ربه ، وإن قال : نعم صنيع الجهال مما تأبى عقول من عرف الرب التفوه به ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم يعارض بمن علم الله أنه لا يقتل ويريدون قتله ويؤثرونه ويقصدون قصده الجميع ما يحتمله ثم يكون على ما علم ، وهذه أسباب لا تجد أحدا يقع النعل به ، وفى الوقوع كذبه ، إلا أن يقول : يمنع ، فيلزمه فى كل من يعلم الله أنه لا يكون المنع مع القوة ، وإذا لزم ذلك لزم الدفع في كل ما يعلم أنه يكون إذا لم يرض به العبد ، فيكون كل خير وشر بالمنع والدفع الذى بهم أن قول خصومهم يؤدى إليه هو الذي حملهم على رأيهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله وما ذكر من الاطلاق والتخلية فهو كلام يتوجه أوجها ثلاثة : رفع العسر
Then it is said to him: What duration did He determine for him? Was it not in the Tablet that He would keep him until that, or that he himself would last until that, or that He would keep him and he would last if he were not killed? If he says the first or the second, then he has claimed falsehood in His report and breach in His promise. If he says the third, it is said: Did He know that he would be killed or not? If he says: No, he deserves separation between his head and his body and eternal punishment from his Lord. If he says: Yes, it is the doing of the ignorant that minds of those who know the Lord refuse to utter, and there is no power except with God. Then he is confronted with the case of one whom God knows will not be killed, and they want to kill him and desire it and intend his intention altogether what he can bear, then it happens according to what He knew. And these are causes that you will not find anyone [unclear] with, and in occurrence it falsifies him, unless he says: 'He prevents.' Then it follows that for everyone whom God knows will not be, there is prevention along with power. And if that follows, then prevention follows in everything that He knows will be if the servant is not pleased with it. Thus, all good and evil are by prevention and repulsion, which is that the statement of their opponents leads to, and it is that which carried them to their opinion. And there is no power except with God. And what was mentioned of absolute free will and abandonment is speech that has three aspects: removal of difficulty.
Source pages p. 882

Commentary
This passage presents the final argument in the exchange. Maturidi poses a dilemma: in the Tablet, did God determine that He would keep the person alive until a certain time, or that the person would live until then, or that He would keep him and he would live if not killed? If the opponent chooses the first two, they accuse God of lying or breaking a promise. If they choose the third, Maturidi asks whether God knew he would be killed. If they say no, that is blasphemy; if yes, then writing a conditional statement is ignorance. Then Maturidi considers the case where God knows someone will not be killed, yet people try to kill him. If the opponent says God prevents the killing, then for everyone God knows will not happen, there is prevention despite ability. This leads to the conclusion that all good and evil occur by God's prevention and permission, which is the very view the opponents were trying to avoid. The mention of 'itlāq wa takhlīya' (absolute free will and abandonment) as a speech with three aspects begins but is cut off, with 'removal of difficulty' as the first aspect.

اللوح
الأجل
الكذب
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The first option
B) The second option
C) The third option
D) None of the options

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's trilemma shows that any attempt to separate God's decree from His knowledge leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an unexpected outcome in your life, do you tend to attribute it to 'God's will' in a way that absolves you of personal responsibility? How does Maturidi's trilemma force you to reconsider that tendency? Will you commit to examining your own choices before resorting to divine decree?
?
If the Preserved Tablet cannot contain a conditional statement about your lifespan, what does that imply about how you should live today? Are you living as if your next moment is guaranteed, or are you truly mindful that your actions matter? What change must you make right now?
?
The trilemma exposes the absurdity of thinking God's knowledge is contingent on human actions. Are there areas of your life where you secretly doubt God's complete knowledge—perhaps thinking He doesn't see your private struggles? How will you realign your heart with the truth that His knowledge encompasses everything, including your choices?

Divine Decree

The Conclusion of Divine Prevention
Maturidi extends his argument to conclude that all events are by divine prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the counterexample of one whom God knows will not be killed to conclude that all good and evil are by God's prevention and permission.

Core Idea
God's knowledge necessitates His control; all outcomes are by His prevention and permission.

After dismantling the opponent's position on the Tablet, Maturidi turns to a concrete scenario: someone whom God knows will not be killed, yet people attempt to kill him. This leads to the inescapable conclusion that everything that occurs is by God's prevention and permission. The passage ends with a brief note on the doctrine of free will and abandonment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the counterexample
Read the passage beginning with 'Then he is confronted with the case of one whom God knows will not be killed'. Notice that despite people's intentions, the outcome matches God's knowledge.
The person is not killed because God knows he will not be killed.
2
Analyse the opponent's escape
The opponent might say that God prevents the killing. Maturidi then generalizes: if prevention occurs in this case, it must occur in every case where God knows something will not happen, given the power to do so.
The key phrase: 'Then it follows that for everyone whom God knows will not be, there is prevention along with power.'
3
Trace the generalization
If prevention applies when God knows something will not happen, it also applies when He knows something will happen that the servant dislikes. Thus, all good and evil are by prevention and repulsion.
This is the conclusion: 'Thus, all good and evil are by prevention and repulsion.'
4
Note the final comment
The passage ends with a remark about 'absolute free will and abandonment' having three aspects, but only 'removal of difficulty' is mentioned before the text cuts off.
This indicates that the discussion of free will is incomplete in the surviving text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And there is no power except with God. And what was mentioned of absolute free will and abandonment is speech that has three aspects: removal of difficulty.

Commentary
This passage presents the final argument in the exchange. Maturidi poses a dilemma: in the Tablet, did God determine that He would keep the person alive until a certain time, or that the person would live until then, or that He would keep him and he would live if not killed? If the opponent chooses the first two, they accuse God of lying or breaking a promise. If they choose the third, Maturidi asks whether God knew he would be killed. If they say no, that is blasphemy; if yes, then writing a conditional statement is ignorance. Then Maturidi considers the case where God knows someone will not be killed, yet people try to kill him. If the opponent says God prevents the killing, then for everyone God knows will not happen, there is prevention despite ability. This leads to the conclusion that all good and evil occur by God's prevention and permission, which is the very view the opponents were trying to avoid. The mention of 'itlāq wa takhlīya' (absolute free will and abandonment) as a speech with three aspects begins but is cut off, with 'removal of difficulty' as the first aspect.

Key Terms
المنع
الدفع
الإطلاق
التخلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opponents believe that God's knowledge does not entail His control over outcomes; humans can override His will.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if God knows something will not happen, then any attempt is futile unless God prevents it, implying that all outcomes are under His control.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw from the case of the person whom God knows will not be killed?
A) Humans have free will to override God's will
B) God's knowledge is limited to what He decrees
C) All good and evil are by God's prevention and repulsion
D) The Preserved Tablet is conditional on human actions

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument forces the opponent to admit that all events are under God's direct prevention and permission.

You believe you control your destiny, but this lesson shows that even your strongest efforts are subject to God's prevention. How would this passage change the way you process that disappointment?
?
The opponent in this passage resisted the idea that all events are by God's prevention. Is there an area of your life—your career, your relationships, your health—where you mentally exclude God's control, believing your will alone decides the outcome? What would it mean for your daily actions if you truly internalized that every outcome is by His permission?
?
This passage ends with 'and there is no power except with God.' When you face a situation that seems hopeless or beyond your ability, do you lean on that truth, or do you rely on your own resources? What is one specific habit you can change this week to consistently acknowledge that all prevention and permission belong to God alone?

Divine Knowledge and the Unchangeable Lifespan
Maturidi asserts God's comprehensive knowledge and corrects misinterpretations of the verse on lifespan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues it is impossible for God's actions to be outside His knowledge and how the verse about the appointed term should be interpreted.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is all-encompassing; He does not alter His decrees like the ignorant.

Maturidi opens with a foundational principle: nothing God does can be outside His knowledge. He then clarifies a verse about the appointed term (ajal), correcting misinterpretations that would imply change in God's decree. This lesson explores his initial reasoning and the proper understanding of divine foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Absorb the opening assertion
Read the first sentence where Maturidi states that it is impossible for God's actions to be outside His knowledge. Notice how he immediately ties this to the intellect.
This principle is the foundation of the entire argument.
2
Examine the two interpretations of the verse
Maturidi presents two interpretations of the verse about lifespan: one that it clarifies the end of a lifespan, and another that it refers to varying lifespans. Identify the nuance between them.
The second interpretation is specifically to counter the idea of God changing His mind.
3
Understand the rejection of change
He rejects the notion that God would alter a lifespan like an ignorant person. Notice how he supports this with the Quranic verse that when the term comes, it cannot be delayed or advanced.
This shows that the appointed term is fixed and known to God.
4
Reflect on the argument against increasing lifespan
Maturidi argues that if God decreed a lifespan, He cannot then increase it through kinship ties, as that would imply a change in His will. He concludes by exalting God above such descriptions.
This section sets up the dilemma that will follow in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أية؛ إذ محال أن يكون ما يفعله خارجا من علمه، والذي قالوا: هو ذلك في المعقول. وللآية قال أهل التأويل: يبين منتهى عمره، وعلى نقصان كل وقت بمضى من عمره. وقال قوم: إنما هو فى مختلف أعمار الخلق من بين مطول ومقصر، لا أن الله يجعل لأحد عمرا ثم تبدو له فيزيد أو ينقص كفعل الجهال في أمورهم على شك، ولا قوة إلا بالله. والله تعالى يقول: فَإِذَا جَاء أَجَلُهُمْ لا يَسْتَأْخِرُونَ سَاعَةٌ وَلَا يَسْتَقْدِمُونَ، وفيما يقول لا يجئ أجلهم بل يقتلون قبل مجيء أجلهم. / والله أيضا لا يزيد في العمر، وكيف يقدر أن يزيد في عمر آخر بصلة الرحم من لم يقدر بابقاء ما ضمن عمره أن يبقيه إلى وقت كذا، بل أقدر عدوه حتى منعه عن ذلك، جل الله وعن هذا الوصف. ثم يقال له: ما ضرب من المدة له ألا كان فى اللوح أنه يبقيه إلى ذلك، أو يبقى هو إلى ذلك، أو يبقيه ويبقى إن لم يقتل؟ فإن قال بالأول والثانى فادعى عليه الكذب فى خبره والخلف فى وعده، وإن قال بالثالث قيل: أكان يعلم يقتل أو لا؟ فإن قال: لا، استحق الإبانة بين رأسه وجسده، والخلود في عذاب ربه، وإن قال: نعم صنيع الجهال مما تأبى عقول من عرف الرب التفوه به، ولا قوة إلا بالله. قيل: لم كتب ما لا يعلم؟ إذ ذلك في العرف يكون منهم لا وسعهم، ثم يعارض بمن علم الله أنه لا يقتل ويريدون قتله ويؤثرونه ويقصدون قصده الجميع ما يحتمله ثم يكون على ما علم، وهذه أسباب لا تجد أحدا يقع النعل به، وفى الوقوع كذبه، إلا أن يقول: يمنع، فيلزمه فى كل من يعلم الله أنه لا يكون المنع مع القوة، وإذا لزم ذلك لزم الدفع في كل ما يعلم أنه يكون إذا لم يرض به العبد، فيكون كل خير وشر بالمنع والدفع الذى بهم أن قول خصومهم يؤدى إليه هو الذي حملهم على رأيهم، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
A verse; for it is impossible that what He does is outside His knowledge, and what they said: that is in the intellect. And regarding the verse, the people of interpretation said: He clarifies the end of his lifespan, and the deficiency of every moment that passes from his lifespan. And a group said: It is only about the varying lifespans of creatures between long and short, not that God assigns a lifespan to anyone and then a new opinion appears to Him to increase or decrease it, like the action of the ignorant in their affairs with doubt, and there is no power except with God. And God Almighty says: 'Then when their term arrives, they will not delay an hour nor advance it,' and in what He says, their term does not come, but rather they are killed before the coming of their term. And God also does not increase in lifespan, and how can He decree to increase another's lifespan through kinship ties for one whom He did not decree to keep alive what He guaranteed of his lifespan to keep him until such a time, but rather He empowered his enemy until he prevented him from that, exalted is God above this description. Then it is said to him: Whatever period of time was set for him, was it not in the Tablet that He would keep him until that, or he would remain until that, or He would keep him and he would remain if not killed? If he says the first or second, then he has accused Him of lying in His report and breaking His promise; and if he says the third, it is said: Did He know he would be killed or not? If he says: No, he deserves separation between his head and his body, and eternal punishment in his Lord's torment; and if he says: Yes, that is the action of the ignorant which the minds of those who know the Lord abhor to utter, and there is no power except with God. It is said: Why did He write what He does not know? For that in custom they would do it without their capacity. Then it is countered by one whom God knows will not be killed and they intend to kill him and they prefer him and they intend his intention, all of them what he can bear, then it happens as He knows; and these are reasons you will not find anyone whose sandal falls off in them, and in the falling is a lie of his, unless he says: He prevents; then it is necessary for him in every one whom God knows that prevention will not occur with power, and if that is necessary, then prevention is necessary in everything that He knows will happen if the servant does not consent to it; then every good and evil would be by prevention and defense, which is that the statement of their opponents leads to, and that is what drove them to their opinion, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 882

Commentary
This passage presents an argument about divine knowledge and the interpretation of a verse concerning the predetermined term (ajal). Maturidi begins by asserting the impossibility of God's actions being outside His knowledge, referring to the verse about the appointed term. He then discusses two interpretations: one that the verse clarifies the end of a lifespan, another that it refers to differing lifespans. He rejects the idea that God changes a lifespan like the ignorant do. He cites the Qur'anic verse that when the term comes, it cannot be delayed or advanced, implying that if someone is killed, it is still within the appointed term. He then addresses an objection: if God decrees a lifespan, could kinship increase it? He presents a dilemma for the objector about whether the decree was recorded in the Preserved Tablet. If the objector says the decree is fixed, he accuses God of lying if it changes; if he says not fixed, then it implies ignorance. Maturidi concludes that such reasoning leads to absurdities, forcing the opponent to admit that all good and evil are by God's prevention and defense, which is exactly what the opponents wanted to avoid. The passage thus defends the coherence of divine foreknowledge and decree.

العلم
الأجل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can change a person's lifespan or that His decrees are subject to alteration.
Correction: Maturidi asserts that God's knowledge is eternal and His decrees are fixed; any suggestion of change would liken Him to the ignorant.

Checkpoint
What is the foundational premise Maturidi establishes at the beginning of this passage?
A) God's actions can sometimes be outside His knowledge.
B) It is impossible for God's actions to be outside His knowledge.
C) The intellect has no role in understanding God's knowledge.
D) The verse about lifespan is only metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge covers all actions, and the appointed term is fixed; He does not alter His decrees like the ignorant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your future, do you genuinely believe that every breath is already decreed by God? Or does a part of you feel that your own efforts can add or subtract from your appointed lifespan? What would change in your daily anxiety if you fully embraced this truth?

?
The passage says God does not alter His decrees like the ignorant. In what areas of your life do you still act as if God might be 'ignorant' of your needs, trying to manipulate outcomes through worry or haste? Will you today surrender that area, truly believing His knowledge is perfect and His decree is enough?

The Dilemma of the Preserved Tablet and the Refutation of Changeable Decrees
Maturidi presents a dilemma about the Preserved Tablet to refute the idea that God's decrees can be changed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma Maturidi poses about the Preserved Tablet and how it leads to the conclusion that all good and evil are by God's prevention and defense.

Core Idea
If God's decree is recorded in the Tablet, it cannot change; otherwise, it implies ignorance.

Maturidi now presents a powerful dilemma for those who claim that human actions can alter God's decrees. He asks about the Preserved Tablet: was the lifespan recorded or not? The answer leads to either accusing God of lying or admitting ignorance. This lesson explores this argument and its ultimate consequence.

Guided Path
1
Set up the dilemma
Read the question Maturidi poses: was the lifespan recorded in the Tablet, and if so, to what extent? Notice the three possibilities he offers.
This forces the opponent to commit to a position.
2
Analyze the responses
Maturidi considers each possible answer: if the first or second, it accuses God of lying; if the third, it leads to questions about God's knowledge of the killing. Work through each branch.
Each response has a devastating rebuttal.
3
Conclude the argument
The opponent is forced to admit that all good and evil are by God's prevention and defense, which is exactly the position they sought to avoid.
This underscores the coherence of divine foreknowledge and decree.

The Three Aspects of Divine Permission (Itlaq and Takhliya)
Maturidi analyzes the three aspects of divine permission and leaving alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three meanings of divine permission (itlaq) and leaving alone (takhliya) in Maturidi's theology, and how they apply to good and evil actions.

Core Idea
Divine permission is not abandonment—it has three distinct meanings.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi clarifies the nuanced concept of divine permission (itlaq) and leaving alone (takhliya). He explains that these terms are not straightforward but have three possible aspects depending on context. This analysis is crucial for understanding how God's will relates to human actions without implying compulsion or neglect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow of the argument: enumeration of three aspects, distinction between good and evil, citation of a Quranic verse, and a contrast between two supplications.
The passage is dense; the translation will help you grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the three aspects of permission
Maturidi states that 'itlaq' and 'takhliya' have three aspects: (1) removal of difficulty and prevention, (2) commanding it, or (3) making it permissible. Find these in the text and note how they differ.
3
Understand the good/evil distinction
Maturidi says that in good actions, these aspects are absolute (mutlaq), but in evil actions they are restricted (muqayyad). He emphasizes that God neither makes good difficult nor compels evil.
4
Contrast the two supplications
Examine the two prayers: 'O God, strengthen us for Your obedience' (praised) vs. 'O God, leave us and our obedience' (not praised). This illustrates the difference between divine help and abandonment.
This contrast proves that divine permission does not equate to leaving the servant alone.
5
Connect to divine decree at the moment of action
Maturidi concludes that when power perishes (i.e., at the time of action), one cannot speak of removal of permission or leaving alone; everything occurs according to divine decree. Reflect on how this preserves both divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكر من الاطلاق والتخلية فهو كلام يتوجه أوجها ثلاثة: رفع العسر والمنع أو الأمر به أو الإباحة، وذلك كله فى الخير مطلق وفى الشر لا إلا مقيدا، لم يعسر ولم يجبر، وإذا كان كذلك فمعارضته بالذي ذكر فاسدة. وما أجاب عنا بالمنع فحق قال الله تعالى في قوله: فَخَلُّوا سَبِيلَهُمْ بعد ذكر المنع وما يحمد من قول الناس: اللهم قونا على طاعتك، ولا يحمد: اللهم خل بيننا وبين طاعتك، ثبت أن لأحدهما حالا ليس للآخر. وكذلك هو يقول بالفعل وقت فناء القدرة، ولا قدرة معه، ولا يقول بارتفاع الإطلاق والتخلية وقت الفعل ليعلم بذلك بعده فيما قدر، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And what was mentioned of absolute permission (itlaq) and leaving alone (takhliya) is speech that has three aspects: removal of difficulty and prevention, or commanding it, or permissibility; and all that is in good is absolute, and in evil is only restricted; He did not make it difficult nor did He compel. And if that is so, then opposing it with what was mentioned is corrupt. And what He answered to us with prevention is true; God Almighty said in His statement: 'So let them go their way' after mentioning prevention. And what is praised from people's speech: 'O God, strengthen us for Your obedience,' and not praised: 'O God, leave us and our obedience,' it is established that for one of them there is a state that is not for the other. And similarly, He says about the act at the time when power perishes, and there is no power with Him, and He does not say about the removal of absolute permission and leaving alone at the time of the act, so that one knows by that afterwards what was decreed, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 882

Commentary
This passage deals with the concepts of 'itlaq' (absolute permission or letting go) and 'takhliya' (leaving alone). Maturidi explains that these terms can have three aspects: (1) removal of difficulty and prevention, (2) commanding it, or (3) making it permissible. In good actions, these are absolute; in evil actions, they are restricted. This nuance contradicts the opponents' claim that God's command 'let them go' implies He has abandoned control. Maturidi then contrasts two forms of prayer: 'O God, strengthen us for Your obedience' (asking for facilitation) versus 'O God, leave us and our obedience' (which is not praised). This shows that there is a difference between divine help and abandonment. He concludes that at the moment of action, when power is exhausted, one cannot speak of removal of permission or leaving alone; rather, everything occurs according to divine decree. The passage thus defends the balance between divine will and human responsibility.

Key Terms
الإطلاق
التخلية
المنع
الإباحة
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's permission (itlaq) or leaving alone (takhliya) means He has no control over human actions.
Correction: Maturidi shows that these terms have multiple meanings (removal of difficulty, command, permission) and are qualified, especially for evil. Moreover, God's command 'let them go' came after a prevention, indicating it is not abandonment. The contrast between the two supplications further proves that divine help (tawfiq) is different from abandonment.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is one of the three aspects of 'itlaq' (absolute permission)?
A) Compulsion (jabr)
B) Removal of difficulty and prevention
C) Abandonment (takhliya)
D) Indifference

Key Takeaway
Divine permission (itlaq) has three meanings, and God's command to 'let them go' is a qualified permission, not abandonment, as shown by the contrast between two supplications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that God has left you alone? How does understanding that divine permission has three distinct aspects challenge that feeling—and what does it demand of you right now?
?
You have likely prayed 'O God, strengthen me for Your obedience'—but do you also regularly act as if God has abandoned you to your own resources? Which prayer truly reflects your daily attitude, and what will you change after this lesson?
?
If God's permission for evil is only restricted and not absolute, then your choice to disobey cannot be blamed on God's 'leaving you.' How does this truth force you to take full ownership of your actions, and what commitment can you make today in light of it?

God's Guarantee of Sustenance Cannot Be Thwarted
Maturidi argues that God's guarantee of sustenance is absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that no creature has the power to prevent God from fulfilling His guarantee of sustenance.

Core Idea
God's guarantee of provision cannot be overridden by anyone.

Maturidi addresses a common theological question: does God's promise of provision imply that humans have no role? He argues that since God guarantees sustenance, no one can prevent it, as that would imply God is under another's power—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Maturidi begins by dismissing a wrong approach to asking about sustenance.
The Arabic includes a Qur'anic proof: 'وَما مِن دابَّةٍ إِلَّا عَلَى اللهِ رِزْقُهَا'.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Maturidi presents a logical dichotomy: either someone has power over God to prevent His guarantee (impossible), or no one does (then no one can truly provide). Find these two cases.
The first possibility leads to a grave conclusion—God being under another's power.
3
Understand the conclusion
Reflect on why both possibilities lead to the same result: God's guarantee is absolute and cannot be prevented by any creature.
This defends God's omnipotence and the certainty of His promise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكلم في سؤال الرزق بوجه لا يرضى به سؤال، بل الوجه في ذلك أن الله تعالى إذ ضمن الرزق بقوله: وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فى الأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللهِ رِزْقُهَا، كان ذلك يملك بملكة أو بما يطعمه. فأما أن يكون لأحد قدرة في الله منع وفاء ما ضمن من الوجه الذي ضمن حتى يلحقه الخلف فى الوعد والعجز عن وفاء ضمنه، فيكون الله فى فعله تحت قدرة غيره، وبغيره يقدر على انجاز الوعد ووفاء العهد، وهذا أمر عظيم، أو لا يكون، فيبطل أن يكون أحد يرزق بما هو في الحقيقة رزق غيره من ذلك الوجه، أو يقدر عليه، ولو كان ذلك فيما القدرة معه لكانت هذه الوحشة تلحقه؛ إذ على أنه ذلك الوجه يطلب رزقه قال: سأل الوراق فقال: يقال لهم: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله مراقبته لله؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أعظموا القول في وصف الأنبياء، إنهم لم يفعلوا ذلك، وإن قالوا: وإن قالوا: نعم، لزمهم القول بها قبل الفعل. نقول له وبالله التوفيق: إن عنيت بالقدرة الأسباب التي هي أحوال القدرة التى تعرض لا محالة لولا التضييع من العبد فبلى، وكل الأنبياء كذلك كانوا، وكذلك الأخيار، وإن أردت به القدرة التى هى مع الفعل أحلت السؤال وصرت كمن يقول: هل راقب الله أحد في ابقاء المعاصى وهو فاعل لها، وذلك مما لا
Then he spoke about the question of sustenance (rizq) in a way that the question is not pleasing; rather, the proper way is that God Almighty, since He guaranteed sustenance by His statement: 'And there is no creature on earth except that upon God is its provision,' that is possessed by possession or by what He feeds it. But if anyone has power over God to prevent the fulfillment of what He guaranteed in the manner He guaranteed, such that failure in the promise and inability to fulfill His guarantee would befall Him, then God would be in His action under the power of another, and by another He would be able to fulfill the promise and keep the covenant—this is a grave matter; or else it is not so, then it would be invalid that anyone provides sustenance that is in reality the provision of another in that manner, or has power over it. And if that were the case where power is with him, then this desolation would befall him; for since in that manner he asks for his sustenance, he said: Al-Warraq asked and said: It is said to them: Did anyone avoid sin of God by observing God? If they say: No, they magnify the statement in describing the prophets, that they did not do that; and if they say: Yes, then they must say it before the act. We say to him, and with God is success: If by capacity (qudra) you mean the causes that are conditions of capacity that occur inevitably were it not for the waste from the servant, then yes, and all the prophets were like that, and likewise the righteous; and if you intend by it the capacity that is with the action, you have misdirected the question and become like one who says: Did God observe anyone in the continuation of sins while He is the doer of them? And that is something that does not.
Source pages p. 883

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of divine sustenance (rizq). Maturidi argues that God's guarantee of provision in the Qur'an means that all creatures are provided for by God. He refutes the idea that anyone has power to prevent God from fulfilling His guarantee, as that would imply God is under someone else's power—an absurdity. If that were not so, then no one could truly provide for another. He then presents a question from al-Warraq regarding whether prophets avoid sin by observing God. The objection is that if one says prophets did not observe God in such a way, it diminishes them; if they say yes, then they must affirm that God's observation precedes the act. Maturidi responds by distinguishing between two meanings of 'capacity' (qudra): 1) the causes that are conditions of capacity, which are present in all prophets and righteous people, and 2) the capacity that is simultaneous with the action, which would make the question incoherent, as it would imply God observes sin while being the doer. The passage thus defends the compatibility of divine provision and human capacity.

Key Terms
الرزق
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans can override God's provision through their own efforts or that God might fail to fulfill His guarantee.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if anyone could prevent God's guarantee, God would be under that person's power, which is impossible. Thus, God's provision is absolute.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi deny the possibility of concerning God's guarantee of sustenance?
A) That God might forget to provide for some creatures.
B) That anyone has power to prevent God from fulfilling His guarantee.
C) That sustenance is earned through human effort alone.
D) That God's provision is limited to believers.

Key Takeaway
God's guarantee of sustenance is absolute and cannot be prevented by any creature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that no one can withhold what God has decreed for you? Examine your heart: when you fear losing your job or income, are you really trusting God’s guarantee, or are you looking at human causes as independent?
?
Think of a time you were anxious about provision. Did your anxiety reflect a hidden belief that someone else had the power to prevent what God promised? Will you recalibrate your trust based on this truth?
?
If you deeply internalized that God’s guarantee of sustenance is absolute and cannot be thwarted, what specific worry would you let go of today? Write it down and commit it to God in prayer, asking Him to replace fear with certainty.

Prophets, Divine Observation, and the Two Meanings of Capacity
Maturidi responds to an objection about prophets and divine observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's resolution of the dilemma about prophets avoiding sin through divine observation, by distinguishing two meanings of capacity (qudra).

Core Idea
Prophets avoid sin by using the capacity God gives them, not by escaping divine observation.

Maturidi presents an objection from al-Warraq: Did prophets avoid sin by observing God? If yes, observation precedes the act; if no, that diminishes the prophets. Maturidi answers by distinguishing between capacity as conditional causes (which prophets have) and capacity simultaneous with the action (which would make the question incoherent).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage containing al-Warraq's question. The dilemma is: if prophets did not avoid sin by God's observation, that belittles them; if they did, then observation would precede the act.
Note the two horns of the dilemma.
2
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi distinguishes two meanings of 'capacity' (qudra). The first is the causes that are conditions of capacity—these are present in prophets. The second is capacity that is with the action itself—this would make the question invalid.
This resolves the dilemma by clarifying what the questioner means.
3
Apply to prophets
Prophets, like all righteous, have the first type of capacity: they use the means God provides to avoid sin.
Thus, they do observe God in the sense of being conscious of Him, but not in a way that implies God compels sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم تكلم في سؤال الرزق بوجه لا يرضى به سؤال، بل الوجه في ذلك أن الله تعالى إذ ضمن الرزق بقوله: وَمَا مِن دَابَّةٍ فى الأَرْضِ إِلَّا عَلَى اللهِ رِزْقُهَا، كان ذلك يملك بملكة أو بما يطعمه. فأما أن يكون لأحد قدرة في الله منع وفاء ما ضمن من الوجه الذي ضمن حتى يلحقه الخلف فى الوعد والعجز عن وفاء ضمنه، فيكون الله فى فعله تحت قدرة غيره، وبغيره يقدر على انجاز الوعد ووفاء العهد، وهذا أمر عظيم، أو لا يكون، فيبطل أن يكون أحد يرزق بما هو في الحقيقة رزق غيره من ذلك الوجه، أو يقدر عليه، ولو كان ذلك فيما القدرة معه لكانت هذه الوحشة تلحقه؛ إذ على أنه ذلك الوجه يطلب رزقه قال: سأل الوراق فقال: يقال لهم: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله مراقبته لله؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أعظموا القول في وصف الأنبياء، إنهم لم يفعلوا ذلك، وإن قالوا: وإن قالوا: نعم، لزمهم القول بها قبل الفعل. نقول له وبالله التوفيق: إن عنيت بالقدرة الأسباب التي هي أحوال القدرة التى تعرض لا محالة لولا التضييع من العبد فبلى، وكل الأنبياء كذلك كانوا، وكذلك الأخيار، وإن أردت به القدرة التى هى مع الفعل أحلت السؤال وصرت كمن يقول: هل راقب الله أحد في ابقاء المعاصى وهو فاعل لها، وذلك مما لا

And if that were the case where power is with him, then this desolation would befall him; for since in that manner he asks for his sustenance, he said: Al-Warraq asked and said: It is said to them: Did anyone avoid sin of God by observing God? And that is something that does not.

Commentary
This passage addresses the issue of divine sustenance (rizq). Maturidi argues that God's guarantee of provision in the Qur'an means that all creatures are provided for by God. Misconception: That prophets are sinless because God compels them or because they are beyond divine observation.

?

?
The prophets used the same kind of capacity you have to avoid sin. What does that imply about your own excuses for disobedience? Are you willing to treat your next temptation as a moment where divine observation makes sin impossible to hide?
?
Is there a sin you have been calling 'inevitable'? How would you live differently if you accepted that the capacity to avoid it is already with you, and it is only your waste that causes it? What concrete step will you take today to use that capacity?

The Two Meanings of Power in Avoiding Sin
Maturidi distinguishes two types of power to resolve a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between power as means (asbab) and power concurrent with the act (qudra ma'a al-fi'l), and how it resolves the dilemma about prophets avoiding sin.

Core Idea
Power to avoid sin is through means, not simultaneous with the act.

Al-Warraq poses a dilemma: if no one can avoid sin by mindfulness, then prophets' sinlessness is overstated; if yes, it implies foreknowledge of sin. Maturidi clarifies by distinguishing two types of power. This lesson unpacks that key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial question and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a question, a dilemma, then Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the two meanings of power
Maturidi presents two possibilities for what 'power' might mean. Find them and note how each changes the answer.
The first meaning is power as means (asbab); the second is power concurrent with the act.
3
See how this answers the objection
Understand why the dilemma is resolved: prophets avoided sin through the first type of power, not the second.
The second type would make God the doer of sins, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سأل الوراق فقال: يقال لهم: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله مراقبته لله؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أعظموا القول في وصف الأنبياء، إنهم لم يفعلوا ذلك، وإن قالوا: نعم، لزمهم القول بها قبل الفعل. نقول له وبالله التوفيق: إن عنيت بالقدرة الأسباب التي هي أحوال القدرة التى تعرض لا محالة لولا التضييع من العبد فبلى، وكل الأنبياء كذلك كانوا، وكذلك الأخيار، وإن أردت به القدرة التى هى مع الفعل أحلت السؤال وصرت كمن يقول: هل راقب الله أحد في ابقاء المعاصى وهو فاعل لها، وذلك مما لا. وهو يعارضك فيقول: هل راقب الله نبى من الأنبياء في ابقاء معصية عامها منه أو أخبرها عنه؟ فمها أجاب فى شيء فمثله الأول. ثم يقال: هل تفضل الله على أحد من أوليائه يمنع قدرة عداوته؟ فإن قال: نعم، نقول: إن الله لم يعط أوليائه قدرة معاصيه، فدايه [؟] فى أعدائه أيضا أنه لم يعطهم قوة طاعته، د إن قال: لا، زعم أنه أعطى أولياءه قرة [؟] عداوته. ومن: إنه لم يعط أعداءه قوة العداوة، فالآن صار إلى أن أعطى أولياءه قوتها وذلك عظيم. ثم بقال: هل أعطى الله وليا قوى على تلك الطاعة حين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، فالوحشة فى طاعة لم يقو عليها ليست بدونها في اجتناب معصية لم يقو عليها، بل قوى على ترك المعصية. وعندهم لم يقو على الطاعة، وهذا أوحش. ثم يقال: هل والى الله ولى أو عاداه، بفعل قوى عليه؟ فإن قال: أقر بالقوة مع الفعل، وإن قال: لا، زعم أن العداوة والولاية بما لا يقوى عليه، وذلك بعيد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Al-Warraq asked and said: It is said to them: Did anyone avoid disobedience to Allah by being mindful of Allah? If they say: No, they exaggerate the description of the prophets, that they did not do that. And if they say: Yes, it would require them to say it before the act. We say to him, and with Allah is success: If you mean by power the means which are the states of power that inevitably occur unless wasted by the servant, then yes, and all the prophets were like that, and likewise the righteous. But if you intend by power that which is with the act, you have made the question invalid and become like one who says: Did Allah watch anyone in the perpetration of sins while He is the doer of them? And that is something which... And he counter-argues saying: Did Allah watch any prophet in the perpetration of a sin done by him or informed about him? Whatever he answers in anything, the same applies to the first. Then it is said: Did Allah bestow favor upon any of His friends by preventing the power of enmity against Him? If he says: Yes, we say: Allah did not give His friends the power of His disobediences, ... then he said: No, he claimed that He gave His friends 'qurra' of enmity against Him. And from: He did not give His enemies the power of enmity, so now he has come to the point that He gave His friends its power, and that is grave. Then it is said: Did Allah give a friend power over that obedience at the time of obedience? If he says: No, then the desolation in an obedience he had no power over is not less than avoiding a disobedience he had no power over, rather he had power to abandon disobedience. And according to them he had no power over obedience, and this is more desolate. Then it is said: Did Allah befriend a friend or show enmity by an act he had power over? If he says: He admits power with the act, and if he says: No, he claims that enmity and friendship are by what he has no power over, and that is far-fetched. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 883

Commentary
This passage records a dialectical exchange with an opponent (al-Warraq). The opponent raises a dilemma: if no one can avoid sin by mindfulness of God, then prophets (who never sinned) would be exaggerated; if yes, then it implies that one can avoid sin before committing it, which is problematic. Maturidi resolves this by distinguishing two meanings of 'power' (qudra): (1) the means (asbab) and states of power that occur naturally unless wasted by the servant—in this sense, yes, prophets and righteous people avoid sin through mindfulness; (2) the power that is concurrent with the act (qudra ma'a al-fi'l)—this would make the question invalid, as it would imply God is the doer of sins. The opponent then counters with a similar question about prophets and sins they are informed about. Maturidi's answer is consistent. Then follow several rounds: about whether God prevents His friends from having power to be His enemies, about giving power for obedience at the time of action, and about whether friendship/enmity is based on acts one has power over. In each case, Maturidi shows that the opponent's position leads to absurdities, while the correct view is that human power is the means (asbab) and that God's giving of power is consistent with justice.

Key Terms
القدرة
الأسباب
المعصية
الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the power to avoid sin is a power that accompanies the act itself, implying God forces obedience.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the power is through means (asbab) that can be wasted; it is not simultaneous with the act.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, how did prophets avoid sin?
A) Through power that accompanies the act of obedience.
B) Through the means (asbab) and states of power that occur unless wasted.
C) Through divine compulsion.
D) They were not able to avoid sin.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi resolves the dilemma by distinguishing power as means from power with the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a temptation to sin, do you rely on a feeling of power at that moment, or on the means God has given you (such as mindfulness, prayer, and avoiding triggers)? How can you shift your reliance to the means?
?
Have you ever felt that avoiding a particular sin is impossible because you lack the strength? How does Maturidi's distinction between power as means and power concurrent with the act challenge that feeling?
?
If you believe that prophets avoided sin through power as means, what does that imply about your own responsibility to use the means God has placed in your life? Are you actively using those means, or are you waiting for a supernatural power that may never come?

Counter-Arguments on Prophets and Enemies
Maturidi counters the opponent's objection and discusses friends and enemies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi defends his position against a counter-argument about prophets and then addresses the issue of God granting power to friends and enemies.

Core Idea
God gives power to friends and enemies in a way consistent with justice.

The opponent counters with a similar question about prophets, and then the discussion shifts to whether God grants power for enmity. Maturidi shows the opponent's position leads to contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-argument
The opponent draws a parallel: did God watch any prophet while sinning? See how Maturidi uses the consistency of his earlier answer.
2
Understand the friend/enemy dilemma
The opponent is asked whether God prevents friends from having power for enmity. Follow the two possible answers and Maturidi's critique.
The unclear words 'فدايه' and 'قرة' appear here.
3
See the absurdity
If the opponent says God does not give friends power for enmity, he implies He gave them 'qurra' of enmity, which is contradictory.
Maturidi concludes this leads to assigning a grievous thing to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سأل الوراق فقال: يقال لهم: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله مراقبته لله؟ فإن قالوا: لا، أعظموا القول في وصف الأنبياء، إنهم لم يفعلوا ذلك، وإن قالوا: نعم، لزمهم القول بها قبل الفعل. نقول له وبالله التوفيق: إن عنيت بالقدرة الأسباب التي هي أحوال القدرة التى تعرض لا محالة لولا التضييع من العبد فبلى، وكل الأنبياء كذلك كانوا، وكذلك الأخيار، وإن أردت به القدرة التى هى مع الفعل أحلت السؤال وصرت كمن يقول: هل راقب الله أحد في ابقاء المعاصى وهو فاعل لها، وذلك مما لا. وهو يعارضك فيقول: هل راقب الله نبى من الأنبياء في ابقاء معصية عامها منه أو أخبرها عنه؟ فمها أجاب فى شيء فمثله الأول. ثم يقال: هل تفضل الله على أحد من أوليائه يمنع قدرة عداوته؟ فإن قال: نعم، نقول: إن الله لم يعط أوليائه قدرة معاصيه، فدايه [؟] فى أعدائه أيضا أنه لم يعطهم قوة طاعته، د إن قال: لا، زعم أنه أعطى أولياءه قرة [؟] عداوته. ومن: إنه لم يعط أعداءه قوة العداوة، فالآن صار إلى أن أعطى أولياءه قوتها وذلك عظيم. ثم بقال: هل أعطى الله وليا قوى على تلك الطاعة حين الطاعة؟ فإن قال: لا، فالوحشة فى طاعة لم يقو عليها ليست بدونها في اجتناب معصية لم يقو عليها، بل قوى على ترك المعصية. وعندهم لم يقو على الطاعة، وهذا أوحش. ثم يقال: هل والى الله ولى أو عاداه، بفعل قوى عليه؟ فإن قال: أقر بالقوة مع الفعل، وإن قال: لا، زعم أن العداوة والولاية بما لا يقوى عليه، وذلك بعيد، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Al-Warraq asked and said: It is said to them: Did anyone avoid disobedience to Allah by being mindful of Allah? If they say: No, they exaggerate the description of the prophets, that they did not do that. And if they say: Yes, it would require them to say it before the act. We say to him, and with Allah is success: If you mean by power the means which are the states of power that inevitably occur unless wasted by the servant, then yes, and all the prophets were like that, and likewise the righteous. But if you intend by power that which is with the act, you have made the question invalid and become like one who says: Did Allah watch anyone in the perpetration of sins while He is the doer of them? And that is something which... And he counter-argues saying: Did Allah watch any prophet in the perpetration of a sin done by him or informed about him? Whatever he answers in anything, the same applies to the first. Then it is said: Did Allah bestow favor upon any of His friends by preventing the power of enmity against Him? If he says: Yes, we say: Allah did not give His friends the power of His disobediences, ... then he said: No, he claimed that He gave His friends 'qurra' of enmity against Him. And from: He did not give His enemies the power of enmity, so now he has come to the point that He gave His friends its power, and that is grave. Then it is said: Did Allah give a friend power over that obedience at the time of obedience? If he says: No, then the desolation in an obedience he had no power over is not less than avoiding a disobedience he had no power over, rather he had power to abandon disobedience. And according to them he had no power over obedience, and this is more desolate. Then it is said: Did Allah befriend a friend or show enmity by an act he had power over? If he says: He admits power with the act, and if he says: No, he claims that enmity and friendship are by what he has no power over, and that is far-fetched. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 883

Commentary
This passage records a dialectical exchange with an opponent (al-Warraq). The opponent raises a dilemma: if no one can avoid sin by mindfulness of God, then prophets (who never sinned) would be exaggerated; if yes, then it implies that one can avoid sin before committing it, which is problematic. Maturidi resolves this by distinguishing two meanings of 'power' (qudra): (1) the means (asbab) and states of power that occur naturally unless wasted by the servant—in this sense, yes, prophets and righteous people avoid sin through mindfulness; (2) the power that is concurrent with the act (qudra ma'a al-fi'l)—this would make the question invalid, as it would imply God is the doer of sins. The opponent then counters with a similar question about prophets and sins they are informed about. Maturidi's answer is consistent. Then follow several rounds: about whether God prevents His friends from having power to be His enemies, about giving power for obedience at the time of action, and about whether friendship/enmity is based on acts one has power over. In each case, Maturidi shows that the opponent's position leads to absurdities, while the correct view is that human power is the means (asbab) and that God's giving of power is consistent with justice.

Key Terms
القدرة
العداوة
الولاية
الأولياء
الأعداء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens if the opponent says God does not prevent friends from having power for enmity?
A) It means God gave friends power for obedience.
B) It implies God gave friends 'qurra' of enmity.
C) It means enemies have power for obedience.
D) It is a consistent position.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that God does not grant friends power for enmity nor enemies power for obedience, preserving divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that because you strive to be a friend of God, He should shield you from all temptation and weakness? What does this passage teach you about the power He gives and the justice behind it? How will you respond when you face a test that makes you feel powerless?
?
When you see people openly opposing God and prospering in their enmity, do you ever doubt that God is fair? This passage shows a consistent divine principle. Where in your heart do you need to replace resentment with trust in God's wisdom?
?
If God does not give His friends power to disobey nor His enemies power to obey, then every ability you have to do good is a gift. Does your gratitude reflect that? What can you do today to acknowledge that every act of obedience is from Him alone?

Power for Obedience and the Basis of Friendship
Maturidi concludes the dialectic on power and divine love/hatred.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's final arguments about power for obedience and the relationship between power and divine friendship/enmity.

Core Idea
Friendship and enmity are based on acts one has power over.

In the final exchange, Maturidi examines whether God grants power for obedience at the very moment of the act, and then questions the basis of divine friendship. He exposes inconsistencies in the opponent's view.

Guided Path
1
Read the questions
Focus on the two final questions: one about power for obedience at the time of obedience, and one about whether friendship/enmity is based on acts one has power over.
2
See the conclusion
Maturidi shows that the opponent's denials lead to absurdity: either admit power with the act or claim friendship/enmity without power.
The lesson ends with 'Nor is there any power except with Allah.'

Who Is More Obedient? The Prophet vs. Iblis
Maturidi compares who is more obedient: one who could sin but refrains, or one who cannot sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument distinguishing between the means for action (al-asbab) and the power that enacts the act (al-quwwa allati ma'a al-fi'l), and how this distinction resolves a comparative question about obedience.

Core Idea
Obedience is not about ability, but about choosing rightly when one has the means to do otherwise.

This passage presents a challenging argument that forces us to think carefully about what we mean by 'ability' (qudra) in the context of divine predestination. Maturidi uses a comparative question between the Prophet and Iblis to uncover a deeper principle about how we evaluate obedience and disobedience. Let's work through it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the two questions: first about who is more praiseworthy, then about who is more obedient.
The passage contains some ambiguous phrases; focus on the overall logic.
2
Identify the main question and the two interpretations of 'ability'
The first question compares the Prophet (who would sin if able) and Iblis (who would obey if able). The response introduces a key distinction: if 'ability' means the means (asbab), the Prophet is more obedient; if it means the power with the act (al-quwwa allati ma'a al-fi'l), the question is mistaken.
This distinction is central to Maturidi's theology of human action.
3
Understand the analogies about water, knowledge, and goodness
The text says a similar example applies to you in water, knowledge, and goodness. Reflect on how these analogies reinforce the same point: the criterion for praise depends on what kind of 'ability' is meant.
The exact reference to water is unclear, but it likely illustrates a similar distinction.
4
See how the second question reinforces the first
The second question asks who is more obedient: one whom God befriends and obeys, or one whom God makes an enemy and disobeys. Whatever answer the opponent gives, it applies to the first question as well, showing consistency.
This final step demonstrates the logical force of Maturidi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : من أَحمدُ ؟ من لو قدر على المعصية عصى، وهو النبي، أو من قدر على الطاعة أطاعه، وهو إبليس . قيل : إن عنيت الأسباب فالأول، وإن عنيت القوة التي معها الفعل أحلت، ومثله عليك فى الماء ومثله عليك في العلم والخير ثم يقال له : من أطوع الله من لو والاه الله أطاعه، أو من لو عاداد بعصاه ؟ فبأى شيء يجيب فى ذلك فهو له فى الأول جواب، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And he said: Who is more praiseworthy? The one who, if he were able to sin, would sin, and he is the prophet; or the one who, if he were able to obey, would obey, and he is Iblis. It was said: If you mean the means, then the first; and if you mean the power with which the act occurs, you have spoken incorrectly. And a similar example applies to you in water, and a similar example applies to you in knowledge and goodness. Then it is said to him: Who is more obedient to God: the one whom God befriends and he obeys, or the one whom God makes an enemy and he disobeys? By whatever thing he answers in that, it is an answer for him in the first, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 884

Commentary
This passage is a distinct argument within the broader discussion, contrasting the relative obedience of the Prophet and Iblis. The question is asked: Who is more praiseworthy (أَحمد) – the prophet who, if he were able to sin, would sin (but in fact is protected), or Iblis who, if he were able to obey, would obey (but in fact disobeys)? The response clarifies that it depends on the interpretation: if by 'ability' one means the external means or preconditions for action (الأسباب), then the prophet is more obedient because he has the means to sin but refrains. If one means the inherent power that accompanies the act itself (القوة التي معها الفعل), then the question is mistaken (أحلت) because that power is given by God and not a criterion for praise. The argument extends a similar analogy to water (perhaps about purity) and to knowledge and goodness. Then it asks a second question: who is more obedient to God – the one whom God befriends and who then obeys, or the one whom God makes an enemy and who then disobeys? Whatever answer the opponent gives, it applies to the first question as well, exposing a consistency. The passage ends with the formula 'there is no power except with God.'

Key Terms
أحمد (more praiseworthy)
قدرة (ability)
الأسباب (means/preconditions)
القوة مع الفعل (power with the act)
طاعة (obedience)
معصية (disobedience)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is a judgment that the Prophet is morally superior to Iblis based on their inherent ability to sin or obey.
Correction: Maturidi is not evaluating their moral worth but using the comparison to expose a logical flaw in how 'ability' is defined. The real issue is whether ability refers to external means (asbab) or the power that enacts the act (al-quwwa allati ma'a al-fi'l). The passage is about the correct understanding of predestination and human agency.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that resolves the question of who is more obedient?
A) Whether one is a prophet or a devil
B) Whether one has free will or not
C) Whether one means the means (أسباب) or the power with the act (القوة مع الفعل)
D) Whether one obeys out of love or fear

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that proper evaluation of obedience depends on whether 'ability' is understood as the means for action or the power that performs it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey God, do you see yourself as one who could disobey but chooses not to? How does that realization change the sincerity of your worship, knowing that your ability to obey is itself a gift from God?
?
The passage exposes the subtle trick of conflating external means with internal power. In your life, have you ever excused a sin by saying 'I couldn't help it,' while ignoring that God had given you the capacity to choose otherwise? How will you respond next time that excuse arises in your heart?
?
If God took away the ease of obedience and made it difficult for you to do good, would you still strive to obey, or would you give up? What does your answer reveal about whether your obedience is truly for God or merely for your own comfort?

The Excuse of Incapability Refuted
Maturidi rebuts the excuse of incapability in both worlds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming 'I cannot do it' is not a valid excuse in either the witnessed or unseen world according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Inability is not an excuse when the very inability is divinely prevented.

Maturidi begins by presenting an opponent's claim that the greatest excuse is lack of ability, applicable to both worldly and unseen obligations. He then delivers a sharp rebuttal: the person is actually 'able in that which prevents ability'—a paradoxical phrase that cuts to the heart of the issue. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence of the Arabic: the opponent says no excuse is greater than saying 'I cannot do it'.
Notice the opponent tries to extend this excuse from the witnessed world to the unseen.
2
Identify the rebuttal
The response begins with 'قيل' and immediately contradicts: the person is able in the very thing that prevents ability. Think about what this means.
This inversion is Maturidi's key argument.
3
Analyze the logic
Maturidi says the person is able in 'what prevents him from ability'—meaning the prevention of ability itself implies an underlying capability that is stopped. Thus, the excuse of inability is self-defeating.
He is not excusing sin; he is showing the opponent's reasoning fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال آخر : إنه لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم أن يقول لو قيل له : لم لا فعلت كذا ؟ فيقول : لأنى لا أقدر عليه، فمثلا في الغائب . قيل : هذا يكون قادرا فيما يمنع عنه القدرة، لا فيها ضيعها باتا وما منع حدوث القدرة . وكذلك أيضا في الشاهد لا عذر أوسع من أن يقول : لم أعلم أمرك ولا نهيك ولا علمت أن فعلى يغضبك، فإن لم يكن عذرا مما أعطى ما لو لم يترك طلبه ليبلغه، فمثله في القوة . وبعد ، فإنه لا عذر على ذلك أيضا أعظم من أن يقول : لأنك أخبرت أنثى لا أفعل، وكذلك علمت فقلت : لو فعلت لصيرتك جاهلا كاذبا، فلم أفعل لهذا، وأن يقول أيضا : لى عليك أعظم المنة؛ الأنك أقدرتني عليه وجعلت أمر ربوبيتك في يدى وأقدرتنى على نقضه، فلى عليك أعظم المنن، وعندك أكثر الأيادي، فمها أجاب من شيء فذلك أعظم منه جوابا له، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And another said: There is no greater excuse for the servant in the witnessed world than to say, if it is said to him: Why did you not do such and such? He says: Because I am not able to do it. So likewise in the unseen. It was said: This one is able in that which prevents him from ability, not in that which he completely wasted, and what prevents the occurrence of ability. And likewise also in the witnessed world, there is no broader excuse than to say: I did not know your command nor your prohibition, nor did I know that my act angers you. And if this is not an excuse, from what was given that if he had not left seeking it, it would have reached him, then similarly in power. And after that, there is no excuse in that also greater than to say: Because you informed me that I will not do it, and likewise you knew and said: If you do it, I would make you ignorant and a liar, so I did not do it for this reason. And he also says: I have a greater favor upon you; because you empowered me over it and placed the matter of your lordship in my hand and empowered me to violate it, so I have the greatest favors upon you, and you have the most benefactions. So whatever he answers with something, that is a greater answer for him, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 884

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer (a refutation) concerning the excuse of inability in both the witnessed (visible, worldly) and unseen (metaphysical or eschatological) realms. The objector claims that no excuse is greater than saying 'I cannot do it,' and that this applies equally in the unseen. The response (by the author) counters that the person is actually able (قادر) in the very thing that prevents him from ability—this is paradoxical. The idea is that if a person is prevented from having ability, he is not truly unable in a blameworthy sense; rather, the prevention of ability itself implies a prior ability that is negated. The text then extends the discussion to the excuse of ignorance: 'I did not know your command or that my act displeases you.' This is also claimed as a broad excuse. The response seems to argue that if such ignorance is not an excuse because the person was given means to know (e.g., he did not actively seek knowledge), then similarly, the lack of power is not an excuse because power was given. Then a further point is added: the servant might say 'You informed me that I will not do it, so I didn't,' attributing his inaction to God's foreknowledge. Or he might claim that God's empowerment itself is a favor, and his using it to disobey is an even greater favor because he had the choice. The author concludes that any answer the opponent gives only strengthens the author's own position. The passage ends with 'there is no power except with God.'

Key Terms
عذر
شاهد
غائب
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that saying 'I cannot do it' is a foolproof excuse for failing obligations.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if the inability is divinely prevented, the person is still considered able in that very context, so the excuse fails.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the person who claims inability?
A) He is completely incapable and excused
B) He is able in that which prevents him from ability
C) He is responsible only in the witnessed world
D) His excuse is valid in the unseen

Key Takeaway
Claiming 'I cannot do it' is not a valid excuse because the very prevention of ability implies an underlying capability for which the servant is accountable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have neglected a religious duty, have you ever said to yourself, 'I just can't do it'? According to this lesson, that very inability may be your own fault. What is one duty you have been avoiding, and what is the real reason you haven't sought the ability to fulfill it? Will you commit today to taking a specific step toward gaining that ability?
?
Do you believe that if God has not given you the capability to change a certain bad habit, you are excused? This lesson says the prevention of ability itself implies you could have been able. Reflect on a habit you think you cannot overcome. Can you honestly say you have not wasted the opportunities to develop self-control? What one action will you take this week to start building that ability?
?
Think of a time you used the excuse 'I can't' to avoid a difficult but important task. After understanding this passage, do you still see that as a valid excuse? How does it change your view of your own responsibility? Write down one specific excuse you have used and the truth behind it.

The Excuse of Ignorance Refuted
Maturidi refutes the excuse of ignorance by analogy with the previous rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ignorance of God's commands and the consequences of actions is not a valid excuse, according to Maturidi.

Core Idea
Ignorance is no excuse if the means to know were available.

After refuting the excuse of inability, Maturidi turns to a second common plea: 'I did not know your command, nor that my act displeases you.' Again, the opponent claims this is the broadest excuse. Maturidi responds by drawing a parallel with the previous argument—if ignorance is not an excuse because the person was given the means to know, then similarly, lack of power is not an excuse because power was given. This lesson examines that analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the second excuse
Read the Arabic: the opponent says there is no broader excuse than 'I did not know your command nor your prohibition'.
This is a common plea of the ignorant.
2
Understand the response
Maturidi says that if this ignorance is not an excuse (because the person was given the means to seek knowledge and neglected it), then similarly, lack of power is not an excuse.
He uses an analogy: both ignorance and inability stem from neglecting what was given.
3
See the logical structure
The argument is a reductio: if you accept that ignorance is an excuse only when one couldn't obtain knowledge, then inability is an excuse only when one couldn't obtain ability. But the opponent grants that ignorance is not an excuse in many cases, so by analogy, inability is also not an excuse.
This ties back to the earlier point about divine prevention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال آخر : إنه لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم أن يقول لو قيل له : لم لا فعلت كذا ؟ فيقول : لأنى لا أقدر عليه، فمثلا في الغائب . قيل : هذا يكون قادرا فيما يمنع عنه القدرة، لا فيها ضيعها باتا وما منع حدوث القدرة . وكذلك أيضا في الشاهد لا عذر أوسع من أن يقول : لم أعلم أمرك ولا نهيك ولا علمت أن فعلى يغضبك، فإن لم يكن عذرا مما أعطى ما لو لم يترك طلبه ليبلغه، فمثله في القوة . وبعد ، فإنه لا عذر على ذلك أيضا أعظم من أن يقول : لأنك أخبرت أنثى لا أفعل، وكذلك علمت فقلت : لو فعلت لصيرتك جاهلا كاذبا، فلم أفعل لهذا، وأن يقول أيضا : لى عليك أعظم المنة؛ الأنك أقدرتني عليه وجعلت أمر ربوبيتك في يدى وأقدرتنى على نقضه، فلى عليك أعظم المنن، وعندك أكثر الأيادي، فمها أجاب من شيء فذلك أعظم منه جوابا له، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And another said: There is no greater excuse for the servant in the witnessed world than to say, if it is said to him: Why did you not do such and such? He says: Because I am not able to do it. So likewise in the unseen. It was said: This one is able in that which prevents him from ability, not in that which he completely wasted, and what prevents the occurrence of ability. And likewise also in the witnessed world, there is no broader excuse than to say: I did not know your command nor your prohibition, nor did I know that my act angers you. And if this is not an excuse, from what was given that if he had not left seeking it, it would have reached him, then similarly in power. And after that, there is no excuse in that also greater than to say: Because you informed me that I will not do it, and likewise you knew and said: If you do it, I would make you ignorant and a liar, so I did not do it for this reason. And he also says: I have a greater favor upon you; because you empowered me over it and placed the matter of your lordship in my hand and empowered me to violate it, so I have the greatest favors upon you, and you have the most benefactions. So whatever he answers with something, that is a greater answer for him, and there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 884

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer (a refutation) concerning the excuse of inability in both the witnessed (visible, worldly) and unseen (metaphysical or eschatological) realms. The objector claims that no excuse is greater than saying 'I cannot do it,' and that this applies equally in the unseen. The response (by the author) counters that the person is actually able (قادر) in the very thing that prevents him from ability—this is paradoxical. The idea is that if a person is prevented from having ability, he is not truly unable in a blameworthy sense; rather, the prevention of ability itself implies a prior ability that is negated. The text then extends the discussion to the excuse of ignorance: 'I did not know your command or that my act displeases you.' This is also claimed as a broad excuse. The response seems to argue that if such ignorance is not an excuse because the person was given means to know (e.g., he did not actively seek knowledge), then similarly, the lack of power is not an excuse because power was given. Then a further point is added: the servant might say 'You informed me that I will not do it, so I didn't,' attributing his inaction to God's foreknowledge. Or he might claim that God's empowerment itself is a favor, and his using it to disobey is an even greater favor because he had the choice. The author concludes that any answer the opponent gives only strengthens the author's own position. The passage ends with 'there is no power except with God.'

Key Terms
عذر
شاهد
علم
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think 'I didn't know' is a universal excuse for wrongdoing.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if knowledge was available, ignorance is culpable; similarly, if ability was available, inability is culpable.

Checkpoint
What condition does Maturidi place on the excuse of ignorance?
A) It is always valid
B) It is valid only in the unseen
C) It is not an excuse if the person had the means to seek knowledge
D) It is only valid if combined with inability

Key Takeaway
Ignorance is not a valid excuse when the person had access to knowledge, just as inability is not a valid excuse when the person had access to ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I didn't know' about a religious obligation, ask yourself: Did I have the means to know? If yes, is that excuse still valid before Allah? What will you do today to seek that knowledge?
?
Maturidi equates ignorance with inability. Consider an area of your life where you have claimed ignorance about a sin. Have you honestly sought the truth? If not, what is stopping you from doing so right now?
?
The passage states that ignorance is no excuse when the means to know were given. Write down one specific excuse you have used for neglecting a command of Allah. Now, burn that excuse (figuratively) and commit to learning what you need to know.

The Excuse of Divine Foreknowledge and Empowerment Refuted
Maturidi refutes excuses based on divine foreknowledge and empowerment, concluding all such arguments backfire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects excuses based on God's foreknowledge and His granting of power as self-defeating.

Core Idea
Any excuse the servant offers only strengthens the argument against him.

Maturidi now tackles the most subtle excuses: the servant might say 'You told me I would not do it, so I didn't' or 'You gave me power over Your commands, so I have a favor over You.' Maturidi concludes that every such answer actually backfires, making the opponent's position weaker. This lesson explores these final objections and the powerful closing statement.

Guided Path
1
Read the final objections
Read the Arabic where the servant offers excuses based on God's foreknowledge and God's empowerment.
These are deeper theological pleas: predestination and divine delegation.
2
Understand Maturidi's response structure
Maturidi says that each answer the servant gives is 'a greater answer for him'—meaning it proves God's case against him.
The phrase 'فمها أجاب من شيء فذلك أعظم منه جوابا له' is key.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله', which is a declaration of dependence on God.
This is a standard closing, but here it underscores that all power belongs to God.
